<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Elizabeth I</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Queen of England and Ireland from 1558 to 1603</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Elizabeth of England" and "Elizabeth Tudor" redirect here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_(disambiguation)" title="Elizabeth I (disambiguation)">Elizabeth I (disambiguation)</a>, <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_England_(disambiguation)" title="Elizabeth of England (disambiguation)">Elizabeth of England (disambiguation)</a>, and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Tudor_(disambiguation)" title="Elizabeth Tudor (disambiguation)">Elizabeth Tudor (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vcard"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above fn" colspan="2" style="background-color: #cbe; font-size: 125%">Elizabeth I</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-image photo" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Darnley_stage_3.jpg"><img alt="Full-length portrait of Queen Elizabeth in her early 40s. <p><b>Elizabeth I</b> (7 September 1533 – 24 March 1603)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[a]</a></sup> was <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">Queen of England</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Irish_monarchs" title="List of Irish monarchs">Ireland</a> from 17 November 1558 until her death in 1603. Sometimes referred to as <b>the Virgin Queen</b>, Elizabeth was the last of the five monarchs of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">House of Tudor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elizabeth was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>, his second wife, who was executed when Elizabeth was two years old. Anne's marriage to Henry was annulled, and Elizabeth was declared <a href="/wiki/Royal_bastard" title="Royal bastard">illegitimate</a>. Her half-brother <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI" title="Edward VI">Edward VI</a> ruled until his death in 1553, bequeathing the crown to <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a> and ignoring the claims of his two half-sisters, the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a> and the younger Elizabeth, in spite of <a href="/wiki/Third_Succession_Act" title="Third Succession Act">statute law to the contrary</a>. Edward's will was set aside and Mary became queen, deposing Lady Jane Grey. During Mary's reign, Elizabeth was imprisoned for nearly a year on suspicion of supporting Protestant rebels.
</p><p>Upon her half-sister's death in 1558, Elizabeth succeeded to the throne and set out to rule by good counsel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[b]</a></sup> She depended heavily on a group of trusted advisers led by <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">William Cecil</a>, whom she created 1st <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baron_Burghley" title="Baron Burghley">Baron Burghley</a>. One of her first actions as queen was the establishment of an English Protestant church, of which she became the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_governor" title="Supreme governor">supreme governor</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a> was to evolve into the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. It was expected that Elizabeth would marry and produce an heir; however, despite numerous courtships, she never did. She was eventually succeeded by her first cousin twice removed, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a>; this laid the foundation for the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>. She had earlier been reluctantly responsible for the imprisonment and execution of James's mother, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>.
</p><p>In government, Elizabeth was more moderate than her father and half-siblings had been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starkey5_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-starkey5-5">[3]</a></sup> One of her mottoes was <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"video et taceo"</i></span> ("I see and keep silent").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[4]</a></sup> In religion, she was relatively tolerant and avoided systematic persecution. After the pope <a href="/wiki/Regnans_in_Excelsis" title="Regnans in Excelsis">declared her illegitimate</a> in 1570 and released her subjects from obedience to her, several conspiracies threatened her life, all of which were defeated with the help of her ministers' secret service, run by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Walsingham" title="Francis Walsingham">Francis Walsingham</a>. Elizabeth was cautious in foreign affairs, manoeuvring between the major powers of <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">France</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Kingdom" title="Spanish Kingdom">Spain</a>. She only half-heartedly supported a number of ineffective, poorly resourced military campaigns in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>, France, and Ireland. By the mid-1580s, England could no longer avoid <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Spanish_War_(1585%E2%80%931604)" title="Anglo-Spanish War (1585–1604)">war with Spain</a>.
</p><p>As she grew older, Elizabeth became celebrated for her <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">virginity</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">cult of personality</a> grew around her which was celebrated in the portraits, pageants, and literature of the day. Elizabeth's reign became known as the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan era</a>. The period is famous for the flourishing of <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance_theatre" title="English Renaissance theatre">English drama</a>, led by playwrights such as <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, and for the prowess of English maritime adventurers such as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_Raleigh" title="Walter Raleigh">Walter Raleigh</a>. Some historians depict Elizabeth as a short-tempered, sometimes indecisive ruler,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[5]</a></sup> who enjoyed more than her fair share of luck. Towards the end of her reign, a series of economic and military problems weakened her popularity. Elizabeth is acknowledged as a charismatic performer ("Gloriana") and a dogged survivor ("Good Queen Bess") in an era when government was ramshackle and limited, and when monarchs in neighbouring countries faced internal problems that jeopardised their thrones. After the short reigns of her half-siblings, her 44 years on the throne provided welcome stability for the kingdom and helped to forge a sense of national identity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starkey5_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-starkey5-5">[3]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="887" data-file-width="1282" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg/290px-HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg/435px-HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg/580px-HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HenryAndAnneBoleynPortraits.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth's parents, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>. Anne was executed within three years of Elizabeth's birth.</div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth was born at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenwich_Palace" title="Greenwich Palace">Greenwich Palace</a> on 7 September 1533 and was named after her grandmothers, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lady_Elizabeth_Howard" title="Lady Elizabeth Howard">Lady Elizabeth Howard</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup> She was the second child of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> born in wedlock to survive infancy. Her mother was Henry's second wife, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>. At birth, Elizabeth was the <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a> to the English throne. Her elder half-sister <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a> had lost her position as a legitimate heir when Henry annulled his marriage to Mary's mother, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, to marry Anne, with the intent to sire a male heir and ensure the Tudor succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup> She was baptised on 10 September 1533, and her godparents were <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>; <a href="/wiki/Henry_Courtenay,_1st_Marquess_of_Exeter" title="Henry Courtenay, 1st Marquess of Exeter">Henry Courtenay, 1st Marquess of Exeter</a>; <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Stafford,_Duchess_of_Norfolk" title="Elizabeth Stafford, Duchess of Norfolk">Elizabeth Stafford, Duchess of Norfolk</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Wotton,_Marchioness_of_Dorset" title="Margaret Wotton, Marchioness of Dorset">Margaret Wotton, Dowager Marchioness of Dorset</a>. A canopy was carried at the ceremony over the infant by her uncle <a href="/wiki/George_Boleyn,_Viscount_Rochford" title="George Boleyn, Viscount Rochford">George Boleyn, Viscount Rochford</a>; <a href="/wiki/John_Hussey,_1st_Baron_Hussey_of_Sleaford" title="John Hussey, 1st Baron Hussey of Sleaford">John Hussey, 1st Baron Hussey of Sleaford</a>; <a href="/wiki/Lord_Thomas_Howard" title="Lord Thomas Howard">Lord Thomas Howard</a>; and <a href="/wiki/William_Howard,_1st_Baron_Howard_of_Effingham" title="William Howard, 1st Baron Howard of Effingham">William Howard, 1st Baron Howard of Effingham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elizabeth was two years and eight months old when her mother was beheaded on 19 May 1536,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> four months after Catherine of Aragon's death from natural causes. Elizabeth was declared illegitimate and deprived of her place in the royal succession.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[c]</a></sup> Eleven days after Anne Boleyn's execution, Henry married <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Jane Seymour</a>. Queen Jane died the next year shortly after the birth of their son, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward</a>, who was undisputed <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> to the throne. Elizabeth was placed in her half-brother's household and carried the <a href="/wiki/Chrisom" title="Chrisom">chrisom</a>, or baptismal cloth, at his christening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1517" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rare portrait of Elizabeth prior to her accession, attributed to <a href="/wiki/William_Scrots" title="William Scrots">William Scrots</a>. It was painted for her father in <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1546.</div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth's first <a href="/wiki/Governess" title="Governess">governess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Bryan" title="Margaret Bryan">Margaret Bryan</a>, wrote that she was "as toward a child and as gentle of conditions as ever I knew any in my life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catherine_Champernowne" title="Catherine Champernowne">Catherine Champernowne</a>, better known by her later, married name of Catherine "Kat" Ashley, was appointed as Elizabeth's governess in 1537, and she remained Elizabeth's friend until her death in 1565. Champernowne taught Elizabeth four languages: French, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, Italian and Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir19977_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir19977-17">[14]</a></sup> By the time <a href="/wiki/William_Grindal" title="William Grindal">William Grindal</a> became her tutor in 1544, Elizabeth could write English, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, and Italian. Under Grindal, a talented and skilful tutor, she also progressed in French and Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L8_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-L8-18">[15]</a></sup> By the age of 12 she was able to translate her stepmother <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a>'s religious work <i><a href="/wiki/Prayers_or_Meditations" title="Prayers or Meditations">Prayers or Meditations</a></i> from English into Italian, Latin, and French, which she presented to her father as a New Year's gift.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seth_Sanders_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seth_Sanders-19">[16]</a></sup> From her teenage years and throughout her life she translated works in Latin and Greek by numerous classical authors, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Pro_Marcello" title="Pro Marcello">Pro Marcello</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Consolation_of_Philosophy" title="The Consolation of Philosophy">De consolatione philosophiae</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>, a treatise by <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Annals_(Tacitus)" title="Annals (Tacitus)">Annals</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Tacitus" title="Tacitus">Tacitus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seth_Sanders_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Seth_Sanders-19">[16]</a></sup> A translation of Tacitus from <a href="/wiki/Lambeth_Palace" title="Lambeth Palace">Lambeth Palace</a> Library, one of only four surviving English translations from the early modern era, was confirmed as Elizabeth's own in 2019, after a detailed analysis of the handwriting and paper was undertaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Grindal died in 1548, Elizabeth received her education under her brother Edward's tutor, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ascham" title="Roger Ascham">Roger Ascham</a>, a sympathetic teacher who believed that learning should be engaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup> Current knowledge of Elizabeth's schooling and precocity comes largely from Ascham's memoirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L8_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-L8-18">[15]</a></sup> By the time her formal education ended in 1550, Elizabeth was one of the best educated women of her generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup> At the end of her life, she was believed to speak the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scottish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish languages</a> in addition to those mentioned above. The Venetian ambassador stated in 1603 that she "possessed [these] languages so thoroughly that each appeared to be her native tongue".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Mark_Stoyle" title="Mark Stoyle">Mark Stoyle</a> suggests that she was probably taught Cornish by <a href="/wiki/William_Killigrew_(Chamberlain_of_the_Exchequer)" title="William Killigrew (Chamberlain of the Exchequer)">William Killigrew</a>, Groom of the Privy Chamber and later Chamberlain of the Exchequer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thomas_Seymour">Thomas Seymour</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="2298" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg/220px-Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg/330px-Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg/440px-Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Seymour_Denizot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth's guardian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Seymour,_1st_Baron_Seymour_of_Sudeley" title="Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley">Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley</a>, may have <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">sexually abused</a> her.</div></div></div>
<p>Henry VIII died in 1547 and Elizabeth's half-brother, Edward VI, became king at the age of nine. Catherine Parr, Henry's widow, soon married <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Seymour,_1st_Baron_Seymour_of_Sudeley" title="Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley">Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley</a>, Edward VI's uncle and the brother of Lord Protector <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset</a>. The couple took Elizabeth into their household at <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a>. There Elizabeth experienced an emotional crisis that some historians believe affected her for the rest of her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-loades2-26">[23]</a></sup> Thomas Seymour engaged in romps and horseplay with the 14-year-old Elizabeth, including entering her bedroom in his nightgown, tickling her, and slapping her on the buttocks. Elizabeth rose early and surrounded herself with maids to avoid his unwelcome morning visits. Parr, rather than confront her husband over his inappropriate activities, joined in. Twice she accompanied him in tickling Elizabeth, and once held her while he cut her black gown "into a thousand pieces".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> However, after Parr discovered the pair in an embrace, she ended this state of affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> In May 1548, Elizabeth was sent away.
</p><p>Thomas Seymour nevertheless continued scheming to control the royal family and tried to have himself appointed the governor of the King's person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup> When Parr died after childbirth on 5 September 1548, he renewed his attentions towards Elizabeth, intent on marrying her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> Her governess <a href="/wiki/Kat_Ashley" title="Kat Ashley">Kat Ashley</a>, who was fond of Seymour, sought to convince Elizabeth to take him as her husband. She tried to convince Elizabeth to write to Seymour and "comfort him in his sorrow",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1997_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1997-32">[29]</a></sup> but Elizabeth claimed that Thomas was not so saddened by her stepmother's death as to need comfort.
</p><p>In January 1549, Seymour was arrested and imprisoned in the Tower on suspicion of conspiring to depose his brother Somerset as Protector, marry <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a> to King Edward VI, and take Elizabeth as his own wife. Elizabeth, living at <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_House" title="Hatfield House">Hatfield House</a>, would admit nothing. Her stubbornness exasperated her interrogator, Sir <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tyrwhitt_(courtier)" title="Robert Tyrwhitt (courtier)">Robert Tyrwhitt</a>, who reported, "I do see it in her face that she is guilty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neale_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-neale-33">[30]</a></sup> Seymour was beheaded on 20 March 1549.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reign_of_Mary_I">Reign of Mary I</span></h2>
<p>Edward VI died on 6 July 1553, aged 15. His will ignored the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_Crown_Act_1543" title="Succession to the Crown Act 1543">Succession to the Crown Act 1543</a>, excluded both Mary and Elizabeth from the succession, and instead declared as his heir Lady Jane Grey, granddaughter of Henry VIII's younger sister <a href="/wiki/Mary_Tudor,_Queen_of_France" title="Mary Tudor, Queen of France">Mary</a>. Jane was proclaimed queen by the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_England" title="Privy Council of England">privy council</a>, but her support quickly crumbled, and she was deposed after nine days. On 3 August 1553, Mary rode triumphantly into London, with Elizabeth at her side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[d]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mary_I_of_England,_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain,_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4380" data-file-width="3195" decoding="async" height="356" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mary_I_of_England%2C_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain%2C_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff/lossy-page1-260px-Mary_I_of_England%2C_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain%2C_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mary_I_of_England%2C_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain%2C_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff/lossy-page1-390px-Mary_I_of_England%2C_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain%2C_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mary_I_of_England%2C_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain%2C_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff/lossy-page1-520px-Mary_I_of_England%2C_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain%2C_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mary_I_of_England,_1516-58_and_Philip_II_of_Spain,_1527-98_RMG_BHC2952.tiff" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip</a>, during whose reign Elizabeth was heir presumptive</div></div></div>
<p>The show of solidarity between the sisters did not last long. Mary, a devout <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, was determined to crush the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestant" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> faith in which Elizabeth had been educated, and she ordered that everyone attend Catholic Mass; Elizabeth had to outwardly conform. Mary's initial popularity ebbed away in 1554 when she announced plans to marry <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip of Spain</a>, the son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor_Charles_V" title="Holy Roman Emperor Charles V">Holy Roman Emperor Charles V</a> and an active Catholic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup> Discontent spread rapidly through the country, and many looked to Elizabeth as a focus for their opposition to Mary's religious policies.
</p><p>In January and February 1554, <a href="/wiki/Wyatt%27s_rebellion" title="Wyatt's rebellion">Wyatt's rebellion</a> broke out; it was soon suppressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> Elizabeth was brought to court and interrogated regarding her role, and on 18 March, she was imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>. Elizabeth fervently protested her innocence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup> Though it is unlikely that she had plotted with the rebels, some of them were known to have approached her. Mary's closest confidant, Charles V's ambassador <a href="/wiki/Simon_Renard" title="Simon Renard">Simon Renard</a>, argued that her throne would never be safe while Elizabeth lived; and <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">Lord Chancellor</a> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gardiner" title="Stephen Gardiner">Stephen Gardiner</a>, worked to have Elizabeth put on trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup> Elizabeth's supporters in the government, including <a href="/wiki/William_Paget,_1st_Baron_Paget" title="William Paget, 1st Baron Paget">William Paget, 1st Baron Paget</a>, convinced Mary to spare her sister in the absence of hard evidence against her. Instead, on 22 May, Elizabeth was moved from the Tower to <a href="/wiki/Woodstock,_Oxfordshire" title="Woodstock, Oxfordshire">Woodstock</a>, where she was to spend almost a year under house arrest in the charge of Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bedingfeld" title="Henry Bedingfeld">Henry Bedingfeld</a>. Crowds cheered her all along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades29_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-loades29-41">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[e]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg/220px-Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg/330px-Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg/440px-Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Balustrade_and_steps_Hatfield_House_Old_Palace_Hertfordshire_England.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Old Palace at <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_House" title="Hatfield House">Hatfield House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hertfordshire" title="Hertfordshire">Hertfordshire</a>, where Elizabeth lived during Mary's reign</div></div></div>
<p>On 17 April 1555, Elizabeth was recalled to court to attend the final stages of Mary's <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England#False_pregnancy" title="Mary I of England">apparent pregnancy</a>. If Mary and her child died, Elizabeth would become queen, but if Mary gave birth to a healthy child, Elizabeth's chances of becoming queen would recede sharply. When it became clear that Mary was not pregnant, no one believed any longer that she could have a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup> Elizabeth's succession seemed assured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>King Philip, who ascended the Spanish throne in 1556, acknowledged the new political reality and cultivated his sister-in-law. She was a better ally than the chief alternative, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, who had grown up in France and was betrothed to the <a href="/wiki/Francis_II_of_France" title="Francis II of France">Dauphin of France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> When his wife fell ill in 1558, King Philip sent the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B3mez_Su%C3%A1rez_de_Figueroa_y_C%C3%B3rdoba,_1st_Duke_of_Feria" title="Gómez Suárez de Figueroa y Córdoba, 1st Duke of Feria">Count of Feria</a> to consult with Elizabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> This interview was conducted at <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_House" title="Hatfield House">Hatfield House</a>, where she had returned to live in October 1555. By October 1558, Elizabeth was already making plans for her government. Mary recognised Elizabeth as her heir on 6 November 1558,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup> and Elizabeth became queen when Mary died on 17 November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession">Accession</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3845" data-file-width="2863" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth I in her coronation robes, patterned with <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor roses</a> and trimmed with <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">ermine</a></div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth became queen at the age of 25, and declared her intentions to her council and other peers who had come to Hatfield to swear allegiance. The speech contains the first record of her adoption of the medieval <a href="/wiki/Political_theology" title="Political theology">political theology</a> of the sovereign's "two bodies": the body natural and the <a href="/wiki/Body_politic" title="Body politic">body politic</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>My lords, the law of nature moves me to sorrow for my sister; the burden that is fallen upon me makes me amazed, and yet, considering I am God's creature, ordained to obey His appointment, I will thereto yield, desiring from the bottom of my heart that I may have assistance of His grace to be the minister of His heavenly will in this office now committed to me. And as I am but one body naturally considered, though by His permission a body politic to govern, so shall I desire you all ... to be assistant to me, that I with my ruling and you with your service may make a good account to Almighty God and leave some comfort to our posterity on earth. I mean to direct all my actions by good advice and counsel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>As her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triumphal_progress" title="Triumphal progress">triumphal progress</a> wound through the city on the eve of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Coronation of Elizabeth I of England">coronation ceremony</a>, she was welcomed wholeheartedly by the citizens and greeted by orations and pageants, most with a strong Protestant flavour. Elizabeth's open and gracious responses endeared her to the spectators, who were "wonderfully ravished".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup> The following day, 15 January 1559, a date chosen by her astrologer <a href="/wiki/John_Dee" title="John Dee">John Dee</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IHR_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-IHR-53">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> Elizabeth was crowned and anointed by <a href="/wiki/Owen_Oglethorpe" title="Owen Oglethorpe">Owen Oglethorpe</a>, the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Carlisle" title="Bishop of Carlisle">bishop of Carlisle</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. She was then presented for the people's acceptance, amidst a deafening noise of organs, fifes, trumpets, drums, and bells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup> Although Elizabeth was welcomed as queen in England, the country was still in a state of anxiety over the perceived Catholic threat at home and overseas, as well as the choice of whom she would marry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_settlement">Church settlement</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_(called)_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4427" data-file-width="3407" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_(called)_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Pelican_Portrait" title="Pelican Portrait">Pelican Portrait</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">pelican</a> was thought to nourish its young with its own blood and served to depict Elizabeth as the "mother of the Church of England".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth's personal religious convictions have been much debated by scholars. She was a Protestant, but kept Catholic symbols (such as the <a href="/wiki/Crucifix" title="Crucifix">crucifix</a>), and downplayed the role of sermons in defiance of a key Protestant belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collinson_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-58">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elizabeth and her advisers perceived the threat of a Catholic crusade against heretical England. The queen therefore sought a Protestant solution that would not offend Catholics too greatly while addressing the desires of English Protestants, but she would not tolerate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritan" title="Puritan">Puritans</a>, who were pushing for far-reaching reforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> As a result, the Parliament of 1559 started to legislate for a church based on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England#Reformation" title="Edward VI of England">Protestant settlement of Edward VI</a>, with the monarch as its head, but with many Catholic elements, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vestments" title="Vestments">vestments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a> backed the proposals strongly, but the bill of supremacy met opposition in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, particularly from the bishops. Elizabeth was fortunate that many bishoprics were vacant at the time, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishopric of Canterbury">Archbishopric of Canterbury</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[f]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[g]</a></sup> This enabled supporters amongst peers to outvote the bishops and conservative peers. Nevertheless, Elizabeth was forced to accept the title of <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a> rather than the more contentious title of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supreme_Head" title="Supreme Head">Supreme Head</a>, which many thought unacceptable for a woman to bear. The new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1559" title="Act of Supremacy 1559">Act of Supremacy</a> became law on 8 May 1559. All public officials were to swear an oath of loyalty to the monarch as the supreme governor or risk disqualification from office; the <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> laws were repealed, to avoid a repeat of the persecution of dissenters practised by Mary. At the same time, a new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1559" title="Act of Uniformity 1559">Act of Uniformity</a> was passed, which made attendance at church and the use of an adapted version of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1552_Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="1552 Book of Common Prayer">1552 <i>Book of Common Prayer</i></a> compulsory, though the penalties for <a href="/wiki/Recusancy" title="Recusancy">recusancy</a>, or failure to attend and conform, were not extreme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_question">Marriage question</span></h2>
<p>From the start of Elizabeth's reign it was expected that she would marry, and the question arose to whom. Although she received many offers, she never married and remained childless; the reasons for this are not clear. Historians have speculated that Thomas Seymour had put her off sexual relationships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[60]</a></sup> She considered several suitors until she was about fifty. Her last courtship was with <a href="/wiki/Francis,_Duke_of_Anjou" title="Francis, Duke of Anjou">Francis, Duke of Anjou</a>, 22 years her junior. While risking possible loss of power like her sister, who played into the hands of King Philip II of Spain, marriage offered the chance of an heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[61]</a></sup> However, the choice of a husband might also provoke political instability or even insurrection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robert_Dudley">Robert Dudley</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="440" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png/220px-Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png/330px-Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pair of <a href="/wiki/Portrait_miniature" title="Portrait miniature">miniatures</a> of Elizabeth and Leicester, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1575</span>, by <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a>. Their friendship lasted for over thirty years, until his death.</div></div></div>
<p>In the spring of 1559, it became evident that Elizabeth was in love with her childhood friend <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Robert Dudley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[63]</a></sup> It was said that his wife <a href="/wiki/Amy_Robsart" title="Amy Robsart">Amy</a> was suffering from a "malady in one of her breasts" and that the queen would like to marry Robert if his wife should die.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[64]</a></sup> By the autumn of 1559, several foreign suitors were vying for Elizabeth's hand; their impatient envoys engaged in ever more scandalous talk and reported that a marriage with her <a href="/wiki/Favourite" title="Favourite">favourite</a> was not welcome in England:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> "There is not a man who does not cry out on him and her with indignation ... she will marry none but the favoured Robert."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> Amy Dudley died in September 1560, from a fall from a flight of stairs and, despite the coroner's <a href="/wiki/Inquest" title="Inquest">inquest</a> finding of accident, many people suspected her husband of having arranged her death so that he could marry the queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[h]</a></sup> Elizabeth seriously considered marrying Dudley for some time. However, <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">William Cecil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Throckmorton" title="Nicholas Throckmorton">Nicholas Throckmorton</a>, and some conservative <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_England" title="Peerage of England">peers</a> made their disapproval unmistakably clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[70]</a></sup> There were even rumours that the nobility would rise if the marriage took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>Among other marriage candidates being considered for the queen, Robert Dudley continued to be regarded as a possible candidate for nearly another decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[72]</a></sup> Elizabeth was extremely jealous of his affections, even when she no longer meant to marry him herself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[73]</a></sup> She raised Dudley to the peerage as <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Leicester" title="Earl of Leicester">Earl of Leicester</a> in 1564. In 1578, he finally married <a href="/wiki/Lettice_Knollys" title="Lettice Knollys">Lettice Knollys</a>, to whom the queen reacted with repeated scenes of displeasure and lifelong hatred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup> Still, Dudley always "remained at the centre of [Elizabeth's] emotional life", as historian <a href="/wiki/Susan_Doran" title="Susan Doran">Susan Doran</a> has described the situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[75]</a></sup> He died shortly after the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> in 1588. After Elizabeth's own death, a note from him was found among her most personal belongings, marked "his last letter" in her handwriting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_candidates">Foreign candidates</span></h3>
<p>Marriage negotiations constituted a key element in Elizabeth's foreign policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh2_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-haigh2-85">[77]</a></sup> She turned down the hand of Philip, her half-sister's widower, early in 1559 but for several years entertained the proposal of King <a href="/wiki/Eric_XIV_of_Sweden" title="Eric XIV of Sweden">Eric XIV of Sweden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup> Earlier in Elizabeth's life a Danish match for her had been discussed; <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> had proposed one with the Danish prince <a href="/wiki/Adolf,_Duke_of_Holstein-Gottorp" title="Adolf, Duke of Holstein-Gottorp">Adolf, Duke of Holstein-Gottorp</a>, in 1545, and Edward Seymour, Duke of Somerset, suggested a marriage with <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II_of_Denmark" title="Frederick II of Denmark">Prince Frederick</a> (later Frederick II) several years later, but the negotiations had abated in 1551.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams-89">[81]</a></sup> In the years around 1559 a Dano-English Protestant alliance was considered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup> and to counter Sweden's proposal, King Frederick II proposed to Elizabeth in late 1559.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-adams-89">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="967" data-file-width="844" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg/150px-Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg/225px-Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg/300px-Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fd%27Alen%C3%A7on.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth was engaged for a time to <a href="/wiki/Francis,_Duke_of_Anjou" title="Francis, Duke of Anjou">Francis, Duke of Anjou</a>. The queen called him her "frog", finding him "not so deformed" as she had been led to expect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>For several years she also seriously negotiated to marry Philip's cousin <a href="/wiki/Charles_II,_Archduke_of_Austria" title="Charles II, Archduke of Austria">Charles II, Archduke of Austria</a>. By 1569, relations with the Habsburgs had deteriorated. Elizabeth considered marriage to two French <a href="/wiki/House_of_Valois" title="House of Valois">Valois</a> princes in turn, first <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry, Duke of Anjou</a>, and then from 1572 to 1581 his brother Francis, Duke of Anjou, formerly Duke of Alençon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[84]</a></sup> This last proposal was tied to a planned alliance against Spanish control of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Netherlands" title="Southern Netherlands">Southern Netherlands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup> Elizabeth seems to have taken the courtship seriously for a time, and wore a frog-shaped earring that Francis had sent her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1563, Elizabeth told an imperial envoy: "If I follow the inclination of my nature, it is this: beggar-woman and single, far rather than queen and married".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh2_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-haigh2-85">[77]</a></sup> Later in the year, following Elizabeth's illness with <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Succession_to_Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Succession to Elizabeth I of England">succession question</a> became a heated issue in Parliament. Members urged the queen to marry or nominate an heir, to prevent a civil war upon her death. She refused to do either. In April she <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#Procedure_in_Commonwealth_realms" title="Legislative session">prorogued</a> the Parliament, which did not reconvene until she needed its support to raise taxes in 1566.
</p><p>Having previously promised to marry, she told an unruly House:
</p>
<blockquote><p>I will never break the word of a prince spoken in public place, for my honour's sake. And therefore I say again, I will marry as soon as I can conveniently, if God take not him away with whom I mind to marry, or myself, or else some other great let happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[87]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>By 1570, senior figures in the government privately accepted that Elizabeth would never marry or name a successor. William Cecil was already seeking solutions to the succession problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh2_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-haigh2-85">[77]</a></sup> For her failure to marry, Elizabeth was often accused of irresponsibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[88]</a></sup> Her silence, however, strengthened her own political security: she knew that if she named an heir, her throne would be vulnerable to a coup; she remembered the way that "a second person, as I have been" had been used as the focus of plots against her predecessor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginity">Virginity</span></h3>
<p>Elizabeth's unmarried status inspired a cult of virginity related to that of the Virgin Mary. In poetry and portraiture, she was depicted as a virgin, a goddess, or both, not as a normal woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[90]</a></sup> At first, only Elizabeth made a virtue of her ostensible virginity: in 1559, she told the Commons, "And, in the end, this shall be for me sufficient, that a marble stone shall declare that a queen, having reigned such a time, lived and died a virgin".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hh_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hh-99">[91]</a></sup> Later on, poets and writers took up the theme and developed an <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> that exalted Elizabeth. Public tributes to the Virgin by 1578 acted as a coded assertion of opposition to the queen's marriage negotiations with the Duke of Alençon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup> Ultimately, Elizabeth would insist she was married to her kingdom and subjects, under divine protection. In 1599, she spoke of "all my husbands, my good people".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4271" data-file-width="6142" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg/250px-Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg/375px-Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg/500px-Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Procession_Portrait_of_Elizabeth_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Procession Picture</i>, c. 1600, showing Elizabeth I borne along by her courtiers</div></div></div>
<p>This claim of virginity was not universally accepted. Catholics accused Elizabeth of engaging in "filthy lust" that symbolically defiled the nation along with her body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV of France</a> said that one of the great questions of Europe was "whether Queen Elizabeth was a maid or no".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>A central issue, when it comes to the question of Elizabeth's virginity, was whether the queen ever consummated her love affair with Robert Dudley. In 1559, she had Dudley's bedchambers moved next to her own apartments. In 1561, she was mysteriously bedridden with an illness that caused her body to swell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1587, a young man calling himself <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Dudley" title="Arthur Dudley">Arthur Dudley</a> was arrested on the coast of Spain under suspicion of being a spy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simancas_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-simancas-106">[98]</a></sup> The man claimed to be the illegitimate son of Elizabeth and Robert Dudley, with his age being consistent with birth during the 1561 illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-levin1-107">[99]</a></sup> He was taken to <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a> for investigation, where he was examined by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Englefield" title="Francis Englefield">Francis Englefield</a>, a Catholic aristocrat exiled to Spain and secretary to King Philip II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simancas_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-simancas-106">[98]</a></sup> Three letters exist today describing the interview, detailing what Arthur proclaimed to be the story of his life, from birth in the royal palace to the time of his arrival in Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simancas_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-simancas-106">[98]</a></sup> However, this failed to convince the Spanish: Englefield admitted to King Philip that Arthur's "claim at present amounts to nothing", but suggested that "he should not be allowed to get away, but [...] kept very secure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin1_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-levin1-107">[99]</a></sup> The king agreed, and Arthur was never heard from again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin2_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-levin2-108">[100]</a></sup> Modern scholarship dismisses the story's basic premise as "impossible",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin1_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-levin1-107">[99]</a></sup> and asserts that Elizabeth's life was so closely observed by contemporaries that she could not have hidden a pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-levin2_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-levin2-108">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Mary.2C_Queen_of_Scots"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mary,_Queen_of_Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1190" decoding="async" height="284" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg/220px-Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg/330px-Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg/440px-Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mary_Queen_of_Scots_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The French relatives of <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, considered her the rightful Queen of England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guy,_96–97_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guy,_96–97-110">[102]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth's first policy toward <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> was to oppose the French presence there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[103]</a></sup> She feared that the French planned to invade England and put her Catholic cousin <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, on the throne. Mary was considered by many to be the heir to the English crown, being the granddaughter of Henry VIII's elder sister, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret</a>. Mary boasted being "the nearest kinswoman she hath".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[i]</a></sup> Elizabeth was persuaded to send a force into Scotland to aid the Protestant rebels, and though the campaign was inept, the resulting <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Edinburgh" title="Treaty of Edinburgh">Treaty of Edinburgh</a> of July 1560 removed the French threat in the north.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[j]</a></sup> When Mary returned to Scotland in 1561 to take up the reins of power, the country had an established Protestant church and was run by a council of Protestant nobles supported by Elizabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[106]</a></sup> Mary refused to ratify the treaty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-loades-117">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1563 Elizabeth proposed her own suitor, Robert Dudley, as a husband for Mary, without asking either of the two people concerned. Both proved unenthusiastic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[108]</a></sup> and in 1565 Mary married <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Lord_Darnley" title="Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley">Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley</a>, who carried his own claim to the English throne. The marriage was the first of a series of errors of judgement by Mary that handed the victory to the Scottish Protestants and to Elizabeth. Darnley quickly became unpopular and was murdered in February 1567 by conspirators almost certainly led by <a href="/wiki/James_Hepburn,_4th_Earl_of_Bothwell" title="James Hepburn, 4th Earl of Bothwell">James Hepburn, 4th Earl of Bothwell</a>. Shortly afterwards, on 15 May 1567, Mary married Bothwell, arousing suspicions that she had been party to the murder of her husband. Elizabeth confronted Mary about the marriage, writing to her:
</p>
<blockquote><p>How could a worse choice be made for your honour than in such haste to marry such a subject, who besides other and notorious lacks, public fame has charged with the murder of your late husband, besides the touching of yourself also in some part, though we trust in that behalf falsely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[109]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>These events led rapidly to Mary's defeat and imprisonment in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loch_Leven_Castle" title="Loch Leven Castle">Loch Leven Castle</a>. The Scottish lords forced her to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abdicate" title="Abdicate">abdicate</a> in favour of her son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_VI" title="James VI">James VI</a>, who had been born in June 1566. James was taken to <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a> to be raised as a Protestant. Mary escaped from <a href="/wiki/Loch_Leven_(Kinross)" title="Loch Leven (Kinross)">Loch Leven</a> in 1568 but after another defeat fled across the border into England, where she had once been assured of support from Elizabeth. Elizabeth's first instinct was to restore her fellow monarch; but she and her council instead chose to play safe. Rather than risk returning Mary to Scotland with an English army or sending her to France and the Catholic enemies of England, they detained her in England, where she was imprisoned for the next nineteen years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_cause">Catholic cause</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2940" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg/170px-Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg/255px-Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg/340px-Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Walsingham" title="Francis Walsingham">Francis Walsingham</a>, Elizabeth's <a href="/wiki/Spymaster" title="Spymaster">spymaster</a>, uncovered several plots against her life.</div></div></div>
<p>Mary was soon the focus for rebellion. In 1569 there was a major Catholic <a href="/wiki/Rising_of_the_North" title="Rising of the North">rising in the North</a>; the goal was to free Mary, marry her to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_4th_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 4th Duke of Norfolk">Thomas Howard, 4th Duke of Norfolk</a>, and put her on the English throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loades,_73_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loades,_73-121">[111]</a></sup> After the rebels' defeat, over 750 of them were executed on Elizabeth's orders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[112]</a></sup> In the belief that the revolt had been successful, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> issued a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">bull</a> in 1570, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Regnans_in_Excelsis" title="Regnans in Excelsis">Regnans in Excelsis</a></i>, which declared "Elizabeth, the pretended Queen of England and the servant of crime" to be excommunicated and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heretic" title="Heretic">heretic</a>, releasing all her subjects from any allegiance to her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrath,_69_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath,_69-123">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collinson_p._67_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collinson_p._67-124">[114]</a></sup> Catholics who obeyed her orders were threatened with <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGrath,_69_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath,_69-123">[113]</a></sup> The papal bull provoked legislative initiatives against Catholics by Parliament, which were, however, mitigated by Elizabeth's intervention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[115]</a></sup> In 1581, to convert English subjects to Catholicism with "the intent" to withdraw them from their allegiance to Elizabeth was made a <a href="/wiki/Treason" title="Treason">treasonable</a> offence, carrying the death penalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[116]</a></sup> From the 1570s <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> priests from continental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seminaries" title="Seminaries">seminaries</a> went to England secretly in the cause of the "reconversion of England".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collinson_p._67_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Collinson_p._67-124">[114]</a></sup> Many suffered execution, engendering a cult of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martyrdom" title="Martyrdom">martyrdom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collinson_p._67_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Collinson_p._67-124">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Regnans in Excelsis</i> gave English Catholics a strong incentive to look to Mary as the legitimate sovereign of England. Mary may not have been told of every Catholic plot to put her on the English throne, but from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ridolfi_Plot" title="Ridolfi Plot">Ridolfi Plot</a> of 1571 (which caused Mary's suitor, the Duke of Norfolk, to lose his head) to the <a href="/wiki/Babington_Plot" title="Babington Plot">Babington Plot</a> of 1586, Elizabeth's spymaster Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Walsingham" title="Francis Walsingham">Francis Walsingham</a> and the royal council keenly assembled a case against her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loades,_73_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Loades,_73-121">[111]</a></sup> At first, Elizabeth resisted calls for Mary's death. By late 1586, she had been persuaded to sanction Mary's trial and execution on the evidence of letters written during the Babington Plot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[117]</a></sup> Elizabeth's proclamation of the sentence announced that "the said Mary, pretending title to the same Crown, had compassed and imagined within the same realm divers things tending to the hurt, death and destruction of our royal person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[118]</a></sup> On 8 February 1587, Mary was beheaded at <a href="/wiki/Fotheringhay_Castle" title="Fotheringhay Castle">Fotheringhay Castle</a>, Northamptonshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[119]</a></sup> After the execution, Elizabeth claimed that she had not intended for the signed execution warrant to be dispatched, and blamed her secretary, <a href="/wiki/William_Davison_(diplomat)" title="William Davison (diplomat)">William Davison</a>, for implementing it without her knowledge. The sincerity of Elizabeth's remorse and whether or not she wanted to delay the warrant have been called into question both by her contemporaries and later historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collinson_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-58">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_and_overseas_trade">Wars and overseas trade</span></h2>
<p>Elizabeth's foreign policy was largely defensive. The exception was the English occupation of <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">Le Havre</a> from October 1562 to June 1563, which ended in failure when Elizabeth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> allies joined with the Catholics to retake the port. Elizabeth's intention had been to exchange Le Havre for <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a>, lost to France in January 1558.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[120]</a></sup> Only through the activities of her fleets did Elizabeth pursue an aggressive policy. This paid off in the war against Spain, 80% of which was fought at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades61_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-loades61-131">[121]</a></sup> She knighted <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francis_Drake%27s_Circumnavigation" title="Francis Drake's Circumnavigation">his circumnavigation</a> of the globe from 1577 to 1580, and he won fame for his raids on Spanish ports and fleets. An element of <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a> and self-enrichment drove Elizabethan seafarers, over whom the queen had little control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1071" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg/300px-Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg/450px-Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg/600px-Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth receiving Dutch ambassadors, 1560s, attributed to <a href="/wiki/Levina_Teerlinc" title="Levina Teerlinc">Levina Teerlinc</a></div></div></div>
<p>After the occupation and loss of Le Havre in 1562–1563, Elizabeth avoided military expeditions on the continent until 1585, when she sent an English army to aid the Protestant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_Revolt" title="Dutch Revolt">Dutch rebels</a> against Philip II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh135_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-haigh135-134">[124]</a></sup> This followed the deaths in 1584 of the queen's allies <a href="/wiki/William_the_Silent" title="William the Silent">William the Silent</a>, Prince of Orange, and the Duke of Anjou, and the surrender of a series of Dutch towns to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Farnese,_Duke_of_Parma" title="Alexander Farnese, Duke of Parma">Alexander Farnese, Duke of Parma</a>, Philip's governor of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Netherlands" title="Spanish Netherlands">Spanish Netherlands</a>. In December 1584, an alliance between Philip II and the French <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(French)" title="Catholic League (French)">Catholic League</a> at <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Joinville" title="Treaty of Joinville">Joinville</a> undermined the ability of Anjou's brother, Henry III of France, to counter <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a> domination of the Netherlands. It also extended Spanish influence along the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">channel</a> coast of France, where the Catholic League was strong, and exposed England to invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh135_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-haigh135-134">[124]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antwerp_(1584%E2%80%931585)" title="Siege of Antwerp (1584–1585)">siege of Antwerp</a> in the summer of 1585 by the Duke of Parma necessitated some reaction on the part of the English and the Dutch. The outcome was the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nonsuch" title="Treaty of Nonsuch">Treaty of Nonsuch</a> of August 1585, in which Elizabeth promised military support to the Dutch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[125]</a></sup> The treaty marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Spanish_War_(1585%E2%80%931604)" title="Anglo-Spanish War (1585–1604)">Anglo-Spanish War</a>, which lasted until the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1604)" title="Treaty of London (1604)">Treaty of London</a> in 1604.
</p><p>The expedition was led by Elizabeth's former suitor, the Earl of Leicester. Elizabeth from the start did not really back this course of action. Her strategy, to support the Dutch on the surface with an English army, while beginning secret peace talks with Spain within days of Leicester's arrival in Holland,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[126]</a></sup> had necessarily to be at odds with Leicester's, who wanted and was expected by the Dutch to fight an active campaign. Elizabeth, on the other hand, wanted him "to avoid at all costs any decisive action with the enemy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[127]</a></sup> He enraged Elizabeth by accepting the post of Governor-General from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_States_General" title="Dutch States General">Dutch States General</a>. Elizabeth saw this as a Dutch ploy to force her to accept sovereignty over the Netherlands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[128]</a></sup> which so far she had always declined. She wrote to Leicester:
</p>
<blockquote><p>We could never have imagined (had we not seen it fall out in experience) that a man raised up by ourself and extraordinarily favoured by us, above any other subject of this land, would have in so contemptible a sort broken our commandment in a cause that so greatly touches us in honour ... And therefore our express pleasure and commandment is that, all delays and excuses laid apart, you do presently upon the duty of your allegiance obey and fulfill whatsoever the bearer hereof shall direct you to do in our name. Whereof fail you not, as you will answer the contrary at your utmost peril.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[129]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Elizabeth's "commandment" was that her emissary read out her letters of disapproval publicly before the Dutch Council of State, Leicester having to stand nearby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[130]</a></sup> This public humiliation of her "Lieutenant-General" combined with her continued talks for a separate peace with Spain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[k]</a></sup> irreversibly undermined Leicester's standing among the Dutch. The military campaign was severely hampered by Elizabeth's repeated refusals to send promised funds for her starving soldiers. Her unwillingness to commit herself to the cause, Leicester's own shortcomings as a political and military leader, and the faction-ridden and chaotic situation of Dutch politics led to the failure of the campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[132]</a></sup> Leicester finally resigned his command in December 1587.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Armada">Spanish Armada</span></h3>
