<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2495" data-file-width="2492" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif/lossy-page1-220px-N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif/lossy-page1-330px-N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif/lossy-page1-440px-N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait from 1586 to 1587, by Nicholas Hilliard, around the time of the voyages of Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a></div></div></div>
<p>Meanwhile, Sir Francis Drake had undertaken a major voyage against Spanish ports and ships in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> in 1585 and 1586. In 1587 he made a <a href="/wiki/Singeing_the_King_of_Spain%27s_Beard" title="Singeing the King of Spain's Beard">successful raid</a> on <a href="/wiki/C%C3%A1diz" title="Cádiz">Cádiz</a>, destroying the Spanish fleet of war ships intended for the <i>Enterprise of England</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[134]</a></sup> as Philip II had decided to take the war to England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh138_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-haigh138-146">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 12 July 1588, the Spanish Armada, a great fleet of ships, set sail for the channel, planning to ferry a Spanish invasion force under the Duke of Parma to the coast of southeast England from the Netherlands. A combination of miscalculation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[l]</a></sup> misfortune, and an attack of English <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_ships" title="Fire ships">fire ships</a> on 29 July off <a href="/wiki/Gravelines" title="Gravelines">Gravelines</a>, which dispersed the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">Spanish</a> ships to the northeast, defeated the Armada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[137]</a></sup> The Armada straggled home to Spain in shattered remnants, after disastrous losses on the coast of Ireland (after some ships had tried to struggle back to Spain via the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, and then back south past the west coast of Ireland).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neale300_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-neale300-150">[138]</a></sup> Unaware of the Armada's fate, English militias mustered to defend the country under the Earl of Leicester's command. Leicester invited Elizabeth to inspect her troops at <a href="/wiki/Tilbury" title="Tilbury">Tilbury</a> in Essex on 8 August. Wearing a silver breastplate over a white velvet dress, she addressed them in one of her <a href="/wiki/Speech_to_the_Troops_at_Tilbury" title="Speech to the Troops at Tilbury">most famous speeches</a>:
</p>
<blockquote><p>My loving people, we have been persuaded by some that are careful of our safety, to take heed how we commit ourself to armed multitudes for fear of treachery; but I assure you, I do not desire to live to distrust my faithful and loving people ... I know I have the body but of a weak and feeble woman, but I have the heart and stomach of a king, and of a King of England too, and think foul scorn that Parma or Spain, or any Prince of Europe should dare to invade the borders of my realm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[139]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_(Armada_Portrait).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1248" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg/300px-Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg/450px-Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg/600px-Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_(Armada_Portrait).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait commemorating the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a>, depicted in the background. Elizabeth's hand rests on the globe, symbolising her international power. One of three known versions of the "<a href="/wiki/Armada_Portrait" title="Armada Portrait">Armada Portrait</a>".</div></div></div>
<p>When no invasion came, the nation rejoiced. Elizabeth's procession to a thanksgiving service at <a href="/wiki/Old_St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="Old St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> rivalled that of her coronation as a spectacle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neale300_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-neale300-150">[138]</a></sup> The defeat of the armada was a potent propaganda victory, both for Elizabeth and for Protestant England. The English took their delivery as a symbol of God's favour and of the nation's inviolability under a virgin queen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades61_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-loades61-131">[121]</a></sup> However, the victory was not a turning point in the war, which continued and often favoured Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xucglh_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-xucglh-152">[140]</a></sup> The Spanish still controlled the southern provinces of the Netherlands, and the threat of invasion remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh138_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-haigh138-146">[135]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Walter_Raleigh" title="Walter Raleigh">Walter Raleigh</a> claimed after her death that Elizabeth's caution had impeded the war against Spain:
</p>
<blockquote><p>If the late queen would have believed her men of war as she did her scribes, we had in her time beaten that great empire in pieces and made their kings of figs and oranges as in old times. But her Majesty did all by halves, and by petty invasions taught the Spaniard how to defend himself, and to see his own weakness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[141]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Though some historians have criticised Elizabeth on similar grounds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[m]</a></sup> Raleigh's verdict has more often been judged unfair. Elizabeth had good reason not to place too much trust in her commanders, who once in action tended, as she put it herself, "to be transported with an haviour of vainglory".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1589, the year after the Spanish Armada, Elizabeth sent to Spain the <i><a href="/wiki/English_Armada" title="English Armada">English Armada</a></i> or <i>Counter Armada</i> with 23,375 men and 150 ships, led by Sir Francis Drake as admiral and Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Norris_(soldier)" title="John Norris (soldier)">John Norreys</a> as general. The English fleet suffered a catastrophic defeat with 11,000–15,000 killed, wounded or died of disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51-159">[146]</a></sup> and 40 ships sunk or captured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51-159">[146]</a></sup> The advantage England had won upon the destruction of the Spanish Armada was lost, and the Spanish victory marked a revival of Philip II's naval power through the next decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg/220px-England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg/330px-England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg/440px-England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">sixpence</a> struck 1593 identifying Elizabeth as "<a href="/wiki/By_the_Grace_of_God" title="By the Grace of God">by the Grace of God</a> Queen of England, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_claim_to_the_French_throne" title="English claim to the French throne">France</a>, and Ireland"</div></div></div>
<p>When the Protestant Henry IV inherited the French throne in 1589, Elizabeth sent him military support. It was her first venture into France since the retreat from Le Havre in 1563. Henry's succession was strongly contested by the Catholic League and by Philip II, and Elizabeth feared a Spanish takeover of the channel ports. The subsequent English campaigns in France, however, were disorganised and ineffective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh142_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-haigh142-161">[148]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_Bertie,_13th_Baron_Willoughby_de_Eresby" title="Peregrine Bertie, 13th Baron Willoughby de Eresby">Peregrine Bertie, 13th Baron Willoughby de Eresby</a>, largely ignoring Elizabeth's orders, roamed northern France to little effect, with an army of 4,000 men. He withdrew in disarray in December 1589, having lost half his troops. In 1591, the campaign of John Norreys, who led 3,000 men to <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, was even more of a disaster. As for all such expeditions, Elizabeth was unwilling to invest in the supplies and reinforcements requested by the commanders. Norreys left for London to plead in person for more support. In his absence, a Catholic League army almost destroyed the remains of his army at <a href="/wiki/Craon,_Mayenne" title="Craon, Mayenne">Craon</a>, north-west France, in May 1591. In July, Elizabeth sent out another force under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex</a>, to help Henry IV in besieging <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>. The result was just as dismal. Essex accomplished nothing and returned home in January 1592. Henry abandoned the siege in April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[149]</a></sup> As usual, Elizabeth lacked control over her commanders once they were abroad. "Where he is, or what he doth, or what he is to do," she wrote of Essex, "we are ignorant".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tudor_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Tudor conquest of Ireland">Tudor conquest of Ireland</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="908" data-file-width="1417" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg/250px-The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg/375px-The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg/500px-The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> chieftain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turlough_Luineach_O%27Neill" title="Turlough Luineach O'Neill">O'Neale</a> and the other <a href="/wiki/Kern_(soldier)" title="Kern (soldier)">kerns</a> kneel to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Sidney" title="Sir Henry Sidney">Sir Henry Sidney</a> in submission.</div></div></div>
<p>Although Ireland was one of her two kingdoms, Elizabeth faced a hostile, and in places virtually autonomous,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[n]</a></sup> Irish population that adhered to Catholicism and was willing to defy her authority and plot with her enemies. Her policy there was to grant land to her courtiers and prevent the rebels from giving Spain a base from which to attack England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[152]</a></sup> In the course of a series of uprisings, Crown forces pursued <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scorched-earth" title="Scorched-earth">scorched-earth</a> tactics, burning the land and slaughtering man, woman and child. During a revolt in <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Gerald_FitzGerald,_14th_Earl_of_Desmond" title="Gerald FitzGerald, 14th Earl of Desmond">Gerald FitzGerald, 14th Earl of Desmond</a>, in 1582, an estimated 30,000 Irish people starved to death. The poet and colonist <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a> wrote that the victims "were brought to such wretchedness as that any stony heart would have rued the same".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[153]</a></sup> Elizabeth advised her commanders that the Irish, "that rude and barbarous nation", be well treated, but she or her commanders showed no remorse when force and bloodshed served their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1594 and 1603, Elizabeth faced her most severe test in Ireland during the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War_(Ireland)" title="Nine Years' War (Ireland)">Nine Years' War</a>, a revolt that took place at the height of hostilities with <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Spanish_War_(1585%E2%80%931604)" title="Anglo-Spanish War (1585–1604)">Spain</a>, who backed the rebel leader, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_O%27Neill,_Earl_of_Tyrone" title="Hugh O'Neill, Earl of Tyrone">Hugh O'Neill, Earl of Tyrone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loades,_98_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loades,_98-169">[155]</a></sup> In spring 1599, Elizabeth sent <a href="/wiki/Essex_in_Ireland" title="Essex in Ireland">Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex</a>, to put the revolt down. To her frustration,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[o]</a></sup> he made little progress and returned to England in defiance of her orders. He was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Blount,_8th_Baron_Mountjoy" title="Charles Blount, 8th Baron Mountjoy">Charles Blount, 8th Baron Mountjoy</a>, who took three years to defeat the rebels. O'Neill finally surrendered in 1603, a few days after Elizabeth's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[156]</a></sup> Soon afterwards, a peace treaty was signed between England and Spain.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h3>
<p>Elizabeth continued to maintain the diplomatic relations with the <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a> that were originally established by her half-brother, Edward VI. She often wrote to Tsar <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan the Terrible</a> on amicable terms, though the Tsar was often annoyed by her focus on commerce rather than on the possibility of a military alliance. Ivan even proposed to her once, and during his later reign, asked for a guarantee to be granted asylum in England should his rule be jeopardised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muscovy_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-muscovy-172">[157]</a></sup> English merchant and explorer <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Jenkinson" title="Anthony Jenkinson">Anthony Jenkinson</a>, who began his career as a representative of the <a href="/wiki/Muscovy_Company" title="Muscovy Company">Muscovy Company</a>, became the queen's <a href="/wiki/List_of_ambassadors_of_the_Kingdom_of_England_to_Russia" title="List of ambassadors of the Kingdom of England to Russia">special ambassador</a> to the court of Tsar Ivan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[158]</a></sup> Upon his death in 1584, Ivan was succeeded by his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feodor_I" title="Feodor I">Feodor I</a>. Unlike his father, Feodor had no enthusiasm in maintaining exclusive trading rights with England. He declared his kingdom open to all foreigners, and dismissed the English ambassador Sir <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Bowes" title="Jerome Bowes">Jerome Bowes</a>, whose pomposity had been tolerated by Ivan. Elizabeth sent a new ambassador, Dr. Giles Fletcher, to demand from the regent <a href="/wiki/Boris_Godunov" title="Boris Godunov">Boris Godunov</a> that he convince the Tsar to reconsider. The negotiations failed, due to Fletcher addressing Feodor with two of his many titles omitted. Elizabeth continued to appeal to Feodor in half appealing, half reproachful letters. She proposed an alliance, something which she had refused to do when offered one by Feodor's father, but was turned down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-muscovy_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-muscovy-172">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muslim_states">Muslim states</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="772" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg/170px-MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg/255px-MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg/340px-MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Abd el-Ouahed ben Messaoud was the Moorish ambassador to Elizabeth in 1600.</div></div></div>
<p>Trade and diplomatic relations developed between England and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbary_states" title="Barbary states">Barbary states</a> during the rule of Elizabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaughan_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-174">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[160]</a></sup> England established a trading relationship with <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> in opposition to Spain, selling armour, ammunition, timber, and metal in exchange for Moroccan sugar, in spite of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papal" title="Papal">papal</a> ban.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[161]</a></sup> In 1600, <a href="/wiki/Abd_el-Ouahed_ben_Messaoud" title="Abd el-Ouahed ben Messaoud">Abd el-Ouahed ben Messaoud</a>, the principal secretary to the Moroccan ruler Mulai <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_al-Mansur" title="Ahmad al-Mansur">Ahmad al-Mansur</a>, visited England as an ambassador to the English court,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaughan_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-174">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[162]</a></sup> to negotiate an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Moroccan_alliance" title="Anglo-Moroccan alliance">Anglo-Moroccan alliance</a> against Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tate.org.uk_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-tate.org.uk-178">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vaughan_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-174">[159]</a></sup> Elizabeth "agreed to sell munitions supplies to Morocco, and she and Mulai Ahmad al-Mansur talked on and off about mounting a joint operation against the Spanish".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kupperman39_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kupperman39-179">[164]</a></sup> Discussions, however, remained inconclusive, and both rulers died within two years of the embassy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p>Diplomatic relations were also established with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> with the chartering of the <a href="/wiki/Levant_Company" title="Levant Company">Levant Company</a> and the dispatch of the first English ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_Porte" title="Sublime Porte">Sublime Porte</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Harborne" title="William Harborne">William Harborne</a>, in 1578.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kupperman39_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kupperman39-179">[164]</a></sup> For the first time, a Treaty of Commerce was signed in 1580.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[166]</a></sup> Numerous envoys were dispatched in both directions and epistolar exchanges occurred between Elizabeth and Sultan <a href="/wiki/Murad_III" title="Murad III">Murad III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kupperman39_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kupperman39-179">[164]</a></sup> In one correspondence, Murad entertained the notion that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islam_and_Protestantism" title="Islam and Protestantism">Islam and Protestantism</a> had "much more in common than either did with Roman Catholicism, as both rejected the worship of idols", and argued for an alliance between England and the Ottoman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[167]</a></sup> To the dismay of Catholic Europe, England exported tin and lead (for cannon-casting) and ammunitions to the Ottoman Empire, and Elizabeth seriously discussed joint military operations with Murad III during the outbreak of war with Spain in 1585, as Francis Walsingham was lobbying for a direct Ottoman military involvement against the common Spanish enemy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="America">America</span></h3>
<p>In 1583, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Humphrey_Gilbert" title="Sir Humphrey Gilbert">Sir Humphrey Gilbert</a> sailed west to establish a colony in Newfoundland. He never returned to England. Gilbert's half-brother <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Walter_Raleigh" title="Sir Walter Raleigh">Sir Walter Raleigh</a> explored the Atlantic Coast and claimed the territory of <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Virginia</a>, perhaps named in honour of Elizabeth, the "Virgin Queen". This territory was much larger than the present-day state of <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>, extending from <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Carolinas" title="Carolinas">Carolinas</a>. In 1585, Raleigh returned to Virginia with a small group of people. They landed on <a href="/wiki/Roanoke_Island" title="Roanoke Island">Roanoke Island</a>, off present-day <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>. After the failure of the first colony, Raleigh recruited another group and put <a href="/wiki/John_White_(colonist_and_artist)" title="John White (colonist and artist)">John White</a> in command. When Raleigh returned in 1590, there was no trace of the <a href="/wiki/Roanoke_Colony" title="Roanoke Colony">Roanoke Colony</a> he had left, but it was the first English settlement in North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[169]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_India_Company">East India Company</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> was formed to trade in the Indian Ocean region and China, and received its charter from Queen Elizabeth on 31 December 1600. For a period of 15 years, the company was awarded a monopoly on English trade with all countries east of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> and west of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Straits_of_Magellan" title="Straits of Magellan">Straits of Magellan</a>. Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Lancaster" title="James Lancaster">James Lancaster</a> commanded the first expedition in 1601. The Company eventually controlled half of world trade and substantial territory in India in the 18th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span></h2>
<p>The period after the defeat of the Spanish Armada in 1588 brought new difficulties for Elizabeth that lasted until the end of her reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xucglh_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-xucglh-152">[140]</a></sup> The conflicts with Spain and in Ireland dragged on, the tax burden grew heavier, and the economy was hit by poor harvests and the cost of war. Prices rose and the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> fell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-haigh-186">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xucglh_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-xucglh-152">[140]</a></sup> During this time, repression of Catholics intensified, and Elizabeth authorised commissions in 1591 to interrogate and monitor Catholic householders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[173]</a></sup> To maintain the illusion of peace and prosperity, she increasingly relied on internal spies and propaganda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-haigh-186">[171]</a></sup> In her last years, mounting criticism reflected a decline in the public's affection for her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[p]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[q]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Devereaux_essex4.jpg"><img alt="Robert Devereaux, 2nd Earl of Essex" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="652" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Devereaux_essex4.jpg/170px-Devereaux_essex4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Devereaux_essex4.jpg/255px-Devereaux_essex4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Devereaux_essex4.jpg/340px-Devereaux_essex4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Devereaux_essex4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lord Essex was a favourite of Elizabeth I despite his petulance and irresponsibility.</div></div></div>
<p>One of the causes for this "second reign" of Elizabeth, as it is sometimes called,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[176]</a></sup> was the changed character of Elizabeth's governing body, the privy council in the 1590s. A new generation was in power. With the exception of William Cecil, Baron Burghley, the most important politicians had died around 1590: the Earl of Leicester in 1588; Sir Francis Walsingham in 1590; and Sir <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hatton" title="Christopher Hatton">Christopher Hatton</a> in 1591.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[177]</a></sup> Factional strife in the government, which had not existed in a noteworthy form before the 1590s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[178]</a></sup> now became its hallmark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[179]</a></sup> A bitter rivalry arose between <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Earl of Salisbury">Robert Cecil</a>, son of Lord Burghley, with both being supported by their respective adherents. The struggle for the most powerful positions in the state marred the kingdom's politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[180]</a></sup> The queen's personal authority was lessening,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[181]</a></sup> as is shown in the 1594 affair of Dr. Lopez, her trusted physician. When he was wrongly accused by the Earl of Essex of treason out of personal pique, she could not prevent the doctor's execution, although she had been angry about his arrest and seems not to have believed in his guilt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the last years of her reign, Elizabeth came to rely on the granting of monopolies as a cost-free system of patronage, rather than asking Parliament for more subsidies in a time of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[r]</a></sup> The practice soon led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Price-fixing" title="Price-fixing">price-fixing</a>, the enrichment of courtiers at the public's expense, and widespread resentment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[184]</a></sup> This culminated in agitation in the House of Commons during the parliament of 1601.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[185]</a></sup> In her famous "<a href="/wiki/Golden_Speech" title="Golden Speech">Golden Speech</a>" of 30 November 1601 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whitehall_Palace" title="Whitehall Palace">Whitehall Palace</a> to a deputation of 140 members, Elizabeth professed ignorance of the abuses, and won the members over with promises and her usual appeal to the emotions:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Who keeps their sovereign from the lapse of error, in which, by ignorance and not by intent they might have fallen, what thank they deserve, we know, though you may guess. And as nothing is more dear to us than the loving conservation of our subjects' hearts, what an undeserved doubt might we have incurred if the abusers of our liberality, the thrallers of our people, the wringers of the poor, had not been told us!<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[187]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_portrait,_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg"><img alt="Elizabeth I in later years" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1744" data-file-width="1296" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_portrait,_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait attributed to <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger" title="Marcus Gheeraerts the Younger">Marcus Gheeraerts the Younger</a> or his studio, c. 1595</div></div></div>
<p>This same period of economic and political uncertainty, however, produced an unsurpassed literary flowering in England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[188]</a></sup> The first signs of a new literary movement had appeared at the end of the second decade of Elizabeth's reign, with <a href="/wiki/John_Lyly" title="John Lyly">John Lyly</a>'s <i>Euphues</i> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Shepheardes_Calender" title="The Shepheardes Calender">The Shepheardes Calender</a></i> in 1578. During the 1590s, some of the great names of <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a> entered their maturity, including <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>. Continuing into the <a href="/wiki/Jacobean_era" title="Jacobean era">Jacobean era</a>, the English theatre would reach its peak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[189]</a></sup> The notion of a great <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan era</a> depends largely on the builders, dramatists, poets, and musicians who were active during Elizabeth's reign. They owed little directly to the queen, who was never a major patron of the arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>As Elizabeth aged her image gradually changed. She was portrayed as <a href="/wiki/Belphoebe" title="Belphoebe">Belphoebe</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astraea_(mythology)" title="Astraea (mythology)">Astraea</a>, and after the Armada, as <a href="/wiki/Gloriana" title="Gloriana">Gloriana</a>, the eternally youthful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faerie_Queene" title="Faerie Queene">Faerie Queene</a> of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>'s poem. Elizabeth gave Edmund Spenser a pension; as this was unusual for her, it indicates that she liked his work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[191]</a></sup> Her painted portraits became less realistic and more a set of enigmatic <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">icons</a> that made her look much younger than she was. In fact, her skin had been scarred by <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> in 1562, leaving her half bald and dependent on wigs and <a href="/wiki/Skin_whitening" title="Skin whitening">cosmetics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades92_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-loades92-210">[192]</a></sup> Her love of sweets and fear of dentists contributed to severe tooth decay and loss to such an extent that foreign ambassadors had a hard time understanding her speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[193]</a></sup> André Hurault de Maisse, Ambassador Extraordinary from Henry IV of France, reported an audience with the queen, during which he noticed, "her teeth are very yellow and unequal ... and on the left side less than on the right. Many of them are missing, so that one cannot understand her easily when she speaks quickly." Yet he added, "her figure is fair and tall and graceful in whatever she does; so far as may be she keeps her dignity, yet humbly and graciously withal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[194]</a></sup> Sir Walter Raleigh called her "a lady whom time had surprised".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[195]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christoffel_van_Sichem_I,_Elizabeth,_Queen_of_Great_Britain,_published_1601,_NGA_38052.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3046" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Christoffel_van_Sichem_I%2C_Elizabeth%2C_Queen_of_Great_Britain%2C_published_1601%2C_NGA_38052.jpg/220px-Christoffel_van_Sichem_I%2C_Elizabeth%2C_Queen_of_Great_Britain%2C_published_1601%2C_NGA_38052.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Christoffel_van_Sichem_I%2C_Elizabeth%2C_Queen_of_Great_Britain%2C_published_1601%2C_NGA_38052.jpg/330px-Christoffel_van_Sichem_I%2C_Elizabeth%2C_Queen_of_Great_Britain%2C_published_1601%2C_NGA_38052.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Christoffel_van_Sichem_I%2C_Elizabeth%2C_Queen_of_Great_Britain%2C_published_1601%2C_NGA_38052.jpg/440px-Christoffel_van_Sichem_I%2C_Elizabeth%2C_Queen_of_Great_Britain%2C_published_1601%2C_NGA_38052.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christoffel_van_Sichem_I,_Elizabeth,_Queen_of_Great_Britain,_published_1601,_NGA_38052.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Christoffel van Sichem I, Elizabeth, Queen of Great Britain, published 1601</div></div></div>
<p>The more Elizabeth's beauty faded, the more her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courtiers" title="Courtiers">courtiers</a> praised it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loades92_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-loades92-210">[192]</a></sup> Elizabeth was happy to play the part,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[s]</a></sup> but it is possible that in the last decade of her life she began to believe her own performance. She became fond and indulgent of the charming but petulant young Earl of Essex, who was Leicester's stepson and took liberties with her for which she forgave him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lds_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lds-216">[197]</a></sup> She repeatedly appointed him to military posts despite his growing record of irresponsibility. After Essex's desertion of his command in Ireland in 1599, Elizabeth had him placed under house arrest and the following year deprived him of his monopolies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[198]</a></sup> In February 1601, Essex tried to raise a rebellion in London. He intended to seize the queen but few rallied to his support, and he was beheaded on 25 February. Elizabeth knew that her own misjudgements were partly to blame for this turn of events. An observer wrote in 1602: "Her delight is to sit in the dark, and sometimes with shedding tears to bewail Essex."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<p>Elizabeth's senior adviser, Lord Burghley, died on 4 August 1598. His political mantle passed to his son Robert, who soon became the leader of the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[t]</a></sup> One task he addressed was to prepare the way for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Succession_to_Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Succession to Elizabeth I of England">smooth succession</a>. Since Elizabeth would never name her successor, Robert Cecil was obliged to proceed in secret.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[u]</a></sup> He therefore entered into a <a href="/wiki/Secret_correspondence_of_James_VI" title="Secret correspondence of James VI">coded negotiation</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a>, who had a strong but unrecognised claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[v]</a></sup> Cecil coached the impatient James to humour Elizabeth and "secure the heart of the highest, to whose sex and quality nothing is so improper as either needless expostulations or over much curiosity in her own actions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willson,_154_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-Willson,_154-221">[201]</a></sup> The advice worked. James's tone delighted Elizabeth, who responded: "So trust I that you will not doubt but that your last letters are so acceptably taken as my thanks cannot be lacking for the same, but yield them to you in grateful sort".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[202]</a></sup> In historian J. E. Neale's view, Elizabeth may not have declared her wishes openly to James, but she made them known with "unmistakable if veiled phrases".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[203]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:412px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="1182" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg/410px-Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg/615px-Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg/820px-Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg 2x" width="410"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth's funeral cortège, 1603, with banners of her royal ancestors</div></div></div>
<p>The queen's health remained fair until the autumn of 1602, when a series of deaths among her friends plunged her into a severe depression. In February 1603, the death of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catherine_Carey,_Countess_of_Nottingham" title="Catherine Carey, Countess of Nottingham">Catherine Carey, Countess of Nottingham</a>, the niece of her cousin and close friend <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Carey" title="Catherine Carey">Lady Knollys</a>, came as a particular blow. In March, Elizabeth fell sick and remained in a "settled and unremovable melancholy", and sat motionless on a cushion for hours on end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[204]</a></sup> When Robert Cecil told her that she must go to bed, she snapped: "Must is not a word to use to princes, little man." She died on 24 March 1603 at <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Palace" title="Richmond Palace">Richmond Palace</a>, between two and three in the morning. A few hours later, Cecil and the council set their plans in motion and proclaimed James King of England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[205]</a></sup>
</p><p>While it has become normative to record Elizabeth's death as occurring in 1603, following <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">English calendar reform</a> in the 1750s, at the time England observed <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Day" title="New Year's Day">New Year's Day</a> on 25 March, commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Lady_Day" title="Lady Day">Lady Day</a>. Thus Elizabeth died on the last day of the year 1602 in the old calendar. The modern convention is to use the old style calendar for the day and month while using the new style calendar for the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[206]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_(left)_2013_crop2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="662" data-file-width="897" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_(left)_2013_crop2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth as shown on her tomb at Westminster Abbey</div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth's coffin was carried downriver at night to <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Whitehall</a>, on a barge lit with torches. At her funeral on 28 April, the coffin was taken to <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Hearse" title="Hearse">hearse</a> drawn by four horses hung with black velvet. In the words of the chronicler <a href="/wiki/John_Stow" title="John Stow">John Stow</a>:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Westminster was surcharged with multitudes of all sorts of people in their streets, houses, windows, leads and gutters, that came out to see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obsequy" title="Obsequy">obsequy</a>, and when they beheld her statue lying upon the coffin, there was such a general sighing, groaning and weeping as the like hath not been seen or known in the memory of man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1999486_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1999486-229">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Elizabeth was interred in Westminster Abbey, in a tomb shared with her half-sister, Mary I. The Latin inscription on their tomb, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"Regno consortes &amp; urna, hic obdormimus Elizabetha et Maria sorores, in spe resurrectionis"</i></span>, translates to "Consorts in realm and tomb, here we sleep, Elizabeth and Mary, sisters, in hope of resurrection".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Elizabeth_I" title="Cultural depictions of Elizabeth I">Cultural depictions of Elizabeth I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg/220px-Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg/330px-Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg/440px-Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Elizabeth I, painted around 1610, during the first revival of interest in her reign. Time sleeps on her right and Death looks over her left shoulder; two <a href="/wiki/Putto" title="Putto">putti</a> hold the crown above her head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[209]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Elizabeth was lamented by many of her subjects, but others were relieved at her death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ld_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ld-232">[210]</a></sup> Expectations of King James started high but then declined. By the 1620s, there was a nostalgic revival of the cult of Elizabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somerset726_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-somerset726-233">[211]</a></sup> Elizabeth was praised as a heroine of the Protestant cause and the ruler of a golden age. James was depicted as a Catholic sympathiser, presiding over a corrupt court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[212]</a></sup> The triumphalist image that Elizabeth had cultivated towards the end of her reign, against a background of factionalism and military and economic difficulties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[213]</a></sup> was taken at face value and her reputation inflated. <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Goodman" title="Godfrey Goodman">Godfrey Goodman</a>, Bishop of Gloucester, recalled: "When we had experience of a Scottish government, the Queen did seem to revive. Then was her memory much magnified."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1999488_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1999488-236">[214]</a></sup> Elizabeth's reign became idealised as a time when crown, church and parliament had worked in constitutional balance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>The picture of Elizabeth painted by her Protestant admirers of the early 17th century has proved lasting and influential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[216]</a></sup> Her memory was also revived during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, when the nation again found itself on the brink of invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dobson_and_Watson,_258_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dobson_and_Watson,_258-239">[217]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, the Elizabethan legend was adapted to the imperial ideology of the day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ld_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ld-232">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[w]</a></sup> and in the mid-20th century, Elizabeth was a romantic symbol of the national resistance to foreign threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[x]</a></sup> Historians of that period, such as <a href="/wiki/J._E._Neale" title="J. E. Neale">J. E. Neale</a> (1934) and <a href="/wiki/A._L._Rowse" title="A. L. Rowse">A. L. Rowse</a> (1950), interpreted Elizabeth's reign as a golden age of progress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[219]</a></sup> Neale and Rowse also idealised the Queen personally: she always did everything right; her more unpleasant traits were ignored or explained as signs of stress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recent historians, however, have taken a more complicated view of Elizabeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haigh,_183_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh,_183-154">[142]</a></sup> Her reign is famous for the defeat of the Armada, and for successful raids against the Spanish, such as those on Cádiz in 1587 and 1596, but some historians point to military failures on land and at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haigh142_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-haigh142-161">[148]</a></sup> In Ireland, Elizabeth's forces ultimately prevailed, but their tactics stain her record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[221]</a></sup> Rather than as a brave defender of the Protestant nations against Spain and the Habsburgs, she is more often regarded as cautious in her foreign policies. She offered very limited aid to foreign Protestants and failed to provide her commanders with the funds to make a difference abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elizabeth established an English church that helped shape a national identity and remains in place today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[223]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeir1999487_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeir1999487-248">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[225]</a></sup> Those who praised her later as a Protestant heroine overlooked her refusal to drop all practices of Catholic origin from the Church of England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[y]</a></sup> Historians note that in her day, strict Protestants regarded the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Acts of Settlement and Uniformity of 1559</a> as a compromise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[228]</a></sup> In fact, Elizabeth believed that faith was personal and did not wish, as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> put it, to "make windows into men's hearts and secret thoughts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though Elizabeth followed a largely defensive foreign policy, her reign raised England's status abroad. "She is only a woman, only mistress of half an island," marvelled <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_V" title="Pope Sixtus V">Pope Sixtus V</a>, "and yet she makes herself feared by Spain, by France, by <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">the Empire</a>, by all".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somerset1_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-somerset1-256">[231]</a></sup> Under Elizabeth, the nation gained a new self-confidence and sense of sovereignty, as <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> fragmented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somerset726_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-somerset726-233">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[232]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[233]</a></sup> Elizabeth was the first Tudor to recognise that a monarch ruled by popular consent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[z]</a></sup> She therefore always worked with parliament and advisers she could trust to tell her the truth—a style of government that her Stuart successors failed to follow. Some historians have called her lucky;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somerset1_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-somerset1-256">[231]</a></sup> she believed that God was protecting her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[235]</a></sup> Priding herself on being "mere English",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[236]</a></sup> Elizabeth trusted in God, honest advice, and the love of her subjects for the success of her rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[237]</a></sup> In a prayer, she offered thanks to God that:
</p>
<blockquote><p>[At a time] when wars and seditions with grievous persecutions have vexed almost all kings and countries round about me, my reign hath been peacable, and my realm a receptacle to thy afflicted Church. The love of my people hath appeared firm, and the devices of my enemies frustrate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-somerset1_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-somerset1-256">[231]</a></sup></p></blockquote>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dates in this article <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">before 14 September 1752</a> are in the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a> and 1 January is treated as the beginning of the year, even though 25 March was treated as the beginning of the year in England during Elizabeth's life.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"I mean to direct all my actions by good advice and counsel."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An Act of July 1536 stated that Elizabeth was "illegitimate ... and utterly foreclosed, excluded and banned to claim, challenge, or demand any inheritance as lawful heir ... to [the King] by lineal descent".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-36">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Elizabeth had assembled 2,000 horsemen, "a remarkable tribute to the size of her affinity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The wives of Wycombe passed cake and wafers to her until her litter became so burdened that she had to beg them to stop."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[38]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"It was fortunate that ten out of twenty-six bishoprics were vacant, for of late there had been a high rate of mortality among the episcopate, and a fever had conveniently carried off Mary's Archbishop of Canterbury, <a href="/wiki/Reginald_Pole" title="Reginald Pole">Reginald Pole</a>, less than twenty-four hours after her own death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[56]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"There were no less than ten sees unrepresented through death or illness and the carelessness of 'the accursed cardinal' [Pole]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Most modern historians have considered murder unlikely; breast cancer and suicide being the most widely accepted explanations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Coroner" title="Coroner">coroner</a>'s report, hitherto believed lost, came to light in <a href="/wiki/The_National_Archives_(United_Kingdom)" title="The National Archives (United Kingdom)">The National Archives</a> in the late 2000s and is compatible with a downstairs fall as well as other violence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[69]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-113">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On Elizabeth's accession, Mary's <a href="/wiki/Counts_and_dukes_of_Guise" title="Counts and dukes of Guise">Guise</a> relatives had pronounced her Queen of England and had the English arms emblazoned with those of Scotland and France on her plate and furniture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guy,_96–97_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guy,_96–97-110">[102]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By the terms of the treaty, both English and French troops withdrew from Scotland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[105]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-142"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-142">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Elizabeth's ambassador in France was actively misleading her as to the true intentions of the Spanish king, who only tried to buy time for his great assault upon England<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[131]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When the Spanish naval commander, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alonso_de_Guzm%C3%A1n_El_Bueno,_7th_Duke_of_Medina_Sidonia" title="Alonso de Guzmán El Bueno, 7th Duke of Medina Sidonia">Duke of Medina Sidonia</a>, reached the coast near Calais, he found the Duke of Parma's troops unready and was forced to wait, giving the English the opportunity to launch their attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[136]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-155"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-155">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, C. H. Wilson castigates Elizabeth for half-heartedness in the war against Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haigh,_183_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haigh,_183-154">[142]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-165"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-165">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One observer wrote that <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a>, for example, was "as unknown to the English here as the most inland part of Virginia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[151]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-170"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-170">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a letter of 19 July 1599 to Essex, Elizabeth wrote: "For what can be more true (if things be rightly examined) than that your two month's journey has brought in never a capital rebel against whom it had been worthy to have adventured one thousand men".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loades,_98_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Loades,_98-169">[155]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-190"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-190">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This criticism of Elizabeth was noted by Elizabeth's early biographers <a href="/wiki/William_Camden" title="William Camden">William Camden</a> and John Clapham. For a detailed account of such criticisms and of Elizabeth's "government by illusion"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[174]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-192"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-192">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John Cramsie, in reviewing the recent scholarship in 2003, argued "the period 1585–1603 is now recognised by scholars as distinctly more troubled than the first half of Elizabeth's long reign. Costly wars against Spain and the Irish, involvement in the Netherlands, socio-economic distress, and an authoritarian turn by the regime all cast a pall over Gloriana's final years, underpinning a weariness with the queen's rule and open criticism of her government and its failures."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[175]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A Patent of Monopoly gave the holder control over an aspect of trade or manufacture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[183]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The metaphor of drama is an appropriate one for Elizabeth's reign, for her power was an illusion—and an illusion was her power. Like Henry IV of France, she projected an image of herself which brought stability and prestige to her country. By constant attention to the details of her total performance, she kept the rest of the cast on their toes and kept her own part as queen."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[196]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">After Essex's downfall, James VI of Scotland referred to Robert Cecil as "king there in effect".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[200]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cecil wrote to James, "The subject itself is so perilous to touch amongst us as it setteth a mark upon his head forever that hatcheth such a bird".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willson,_154_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willson,_154-221">[201]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-223"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-223">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">James VI of Scotland was a great-great-grandson of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII of England</a>, and thus Elizabeth's first cousin twice removed, since Henry VII was Elizabeth's paternal grandfather.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-240"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-240">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The age of Elizabeth was redrawn as one of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>, epitomised by courtly encounters between the queen and sea-dog "heroes" such as Drake and Raleigh. Some Victorian narratives, such as Raleigh laying his cloak before the queen or presenting her with a potato, remain part of the myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dobson_and_Watson,_258_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dobson_and_Watson,_258-239">[217]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-242"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-242">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his preface to the 1952 reprint of <i>Queen Elizabeth I</i>, J. E. Neale observed: "The book was written before such words as "ideological", "fifth column", and "cold war" became current; and it is perhaps as well that they are not there. But the ideas are present, as is the idea of romantic leadership of a nation in peril, because they were present in Elizabethan times".</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-251"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-251">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The new state religion was condemned at the time in such terms as "a cloaked papistry, or mingle mangle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[226]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-260"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-260">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Elizabeth's <a href="/wiki/Lord_Keeper_of_the_Great_Seal" title="Lord Keeper of the Great Seal">Lord Keeper</a>, Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholas_Bacon_(courtier)" title="Nicholas Bacon (courtier)">Nicholas Bacon</a>, put it on her behalf to parliament in 1559, the queen "is not, nor ever meaneth to be, so wedded to her own will and fantasy that for the satisfaction thereof she will do anything ... to bring any bondage or servitude to her people, or give any just occasion to them of any inward grudge whereby any tumults or stirs might arise as hath done of late days".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[234]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
